(October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> During the Bronze Age, they developed the <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Pegged <a href="/wiki/Mortise_and_tenon" title="Mortise and tenon">mortise-and-tenon</a> joints proved effective enough to serve as a standard until late into the Roman Empire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Phoenicians were possibly the first to introduce the <a href="/wiki/Bireme" title="Bireme">bireme</a>, around 700 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> An Assyrian account describes Phoenicians evading capture with these ships.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Phoenicians are also credited with inventing the <a href="/wiki/Trireme" title="Trireme">trireme</a>, which was regarded as the most advanced and powerful vessel in the ancient Mediterranean world, and was eventually adopted by the Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-:02-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Phoenicians developed several other maritime inventions. The <a href="/wiki/Amphora" title="Amphora">amphora</a>, a type of container used for both dry and liquid goods, was an ancient Phoenician invention that became a standardized measurement of volume for close to two thousand years. The remnants of self-cleaning artificial harbors have been discovered in Sidon, Tyre, Atlit, and Acre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The first example of <a href="/wiki/Admiralty_law" title="Admiralty law">admiralty law</a> also appears in the Levant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The Phoenicians continued to contribute to cartography into the Iron Age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2014, a roughly 50-foot Phoenician trading ship was found near <a href="/wiki/Gozo" title="Gozo">Gozo</a> island in Malta. Dated 700 BC, it is one of the oldest wrecks found in the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Fifty amphorae, used to contain wine and oil, were scattered nearby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Important_cities_and_colonies">Important cities and colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Important cities and colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:642px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1616" data-file-width="2836" decoding="async" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/640px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/960px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg/1280px-Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg 2x" width="640"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Griechischen_und_ph%C3%B6nizischen_Kolonien.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Phoenician (yellow labels) and Greek (red labels) colonies around 8th to 6th century BC (with German legend)</div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Phoenician_cities" title="List of Phoenician cities">List of Phoenician cities</a></div>
<p>The Phoenicians were not a nation in the political sense. However, they were organized into independent city-states that shared a common language and culture. The leading city-states were Tyre, Sidon, and Byblos. Rivalries were expected, but armed conflict was rare.
</p><p>Numerous other cities existed in the Levant alone, many probably unknown, including Beiruta (modern <a href="/wiki/Beirut" title="Beirut">Beirut</a>) Ampi, Amia, Arqa, <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a>, Botrys, Sarepta, and <a href="/wiki/Tripolis_(region_of_Phoenicia)" title="Tripolis (region of Phoenicia)">Tripolis</a>. From the late tenth century BC, the Phoenicians established commercial outposts throughout the Mediterranean, with Tyre founding colonies in Cyprus, Sardinia, Iberia, the Balearic Islands, Sicily, Malta, and North Africa. Later colonies were established beyond the Straits of Gibraltar, particularly on the Atlantic coast of Iberia. The Phoenicians may have explored the Canary Islands and the British Isles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_C._Scott_CCR_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-John_C._Scott_CCR-10">[10]</a></sup> Phoenician settlement was primarily concentrated in Cyprus, Sicily, Sardinia, Malta, northwest Africa, the Balearic Islands, and southern Iberia.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phoenician_colonization">Phoenician colonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Phoenician colonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To facilitate their commercial ventures, the Phoenicians established numerous colonies and trading posts along the coasts of the Mediterranean. Phoenician city states generally lacked the numbers or even the desire to expand their territory overseas. Few colonies had more than 1,000 inhabitants; only Carthage and some nearby settlements in the western Mediterranean would grow larger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> A major motivating factor was competition with the Greeks, who began expanding across the Mediterranean during the same period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClineGraham2011_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClineGraham2011-94">[94]</a></sup> Though largely peaceful rivals, their respective settlements in Crete and Sicily did clash intermittently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SacksMurray2009_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-SacksMurray2009-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest Phoenician settlements outside the Levant were on <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, gradually moving westward towards <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, as well as on the European mainland in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Marseilles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaldmanMason2006_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaldmanMason2006-96">[96]</a></sup> The first Phoenician colonies in the western Mediterranean were along the northwest African coast and on <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Balearic_Islands" title="Balearic Islands">Balearic Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2008_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2008-97">[97]</a></sup> Tyre led the way in settling or controlling coastal areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naylor2009_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naylor2009-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Phoenician colonies were fairly autonomous. At most, they were expected to send annual tribute to their mother city, usually in the context of a religious offering. However, in the seventh century BC the western colonies came under the control of Carthage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SwartzDumett1980_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-SwartzDumett1980-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GarnseyWhittaker2007_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-GarnseyWhittaker2007-100">[100]</a></sup> which was exercised directly through appointed magistrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts20042_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts20042-101">[101]</a></sup> Carthage continued to send annual tribute to Tyre for some time after its independence.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Since very little of the Phoenicians' writings have survived, much of what is known about their culture and society comes from accounts by contemporary civilizations or inferences from archaeological discoveries. The Phoenicians had much in common with other Canaanites, including language, religion, social customs, and a monarchical political system centered around city-states. However, by the early Iron Age (roughly 1300 BC), they had emerged as distinct people. Their culture, economy, and daily life were heavily centered on commerce and maritime trade. Their propensity for seafaring brought them into contact with numerous other civilizations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_government">Politics and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Politics and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1622" data-file-width="2434" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg/220px-King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg/330px-King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg/440px-King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:King_Hiram_Of_Tyre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Hiram_I" title="Hiram I">King Hiram I of Tyre</a>, located in the village of <a href="/wiki/Hanaouay" title="Hanaouay">Hanaouay</a> in southern Lebanon.</div></div></div>
<p>The Phoenician city-states were fiercely independent in both domestic and foreign affairs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Formal alliances between city-states were rare. The relative power and influence of city-states varied over time. <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> was dominant between the 12th and 11th centuries BC and influenced its neighbors. However, by the tenth century BC, <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a> rose to become the most powerful city.
</p><p>At least in its earlier stages, Phoenician society was highly stratified and predominantly <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchical</a>. Hereditary kings usually governed with absolute power over civic, commercial, and religious affairs. They often relied upon senior officials from the noble and merchant classes; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Priesthood" title="Priesthood">priesthood</a> was a distinct class, usually of royal lineage or leading merchant families. The King was considered a representative of the gods and carried many obligations and duties concerning religious processions and rituals. Priests were thus highly influential and often became intertwined with the royal family.
</p><p>Phoenician kings did not commemorate their reign through sculptures or monuments. Their wealth, power, and accomplishments were usually conveyed through ornate sarcophagi, like that of <a href="/wiki/Ahiram_sarcophagus" title="Ahiram sarcophagus">Ahiram</a> of <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>. The Phoenicians kept records of their rulers in tomb inscriptions, which are among the few primary sources still available. Historians have determined a clear line of succession over centuries for some city-states, notably Byblos and Tyre.
</p><p>Starting as early as 15th century BC, Phoenician leaders were "advised by councils or assemblies which gradually took greater power".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-:10-61">[61]</a></sup> In the sixth century BC, during the period of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia_under_Babylonian_rule" title="Phoenicia under Babylonian rule">Babylonian rule</a>, Tyre briefly adopted a system of government consisting of a pair of judges with authority roughly equivalent to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">Roman consul</a>, known as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sufetes</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Shophet" title="Shophet">shophets</a>), who were chosen from the most powerful noble families and served short terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-:10-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:275px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1388" data-file-width="1954" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg/273px-Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg/410px-Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg/546px-Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg 2x" width="273"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phoenician_Merchants_and_Traders.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>19th-century depiction of Phoenician sailors and merchants. The importance of trade to the Phoenician economy led to a gradual sharing of power between the King and assemblies of merchant families.</div></div></div>
<p>In the fourth century BC, when the armies of Alexander the Great approached Tyre, they were met not by its King but by representatives of the commonwealth of the city. Similarly, historians at the time describe the "inhabitants" or "the people" of Sidon making peace with Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-:10-61">[61]</a></sup> When the Macedonians sought to appoint a new king over Sidon, the citizens nominated their candidate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:10_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-:10-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_administration">Law and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Law and administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the King and council, the two most important political positions in virtually every Phoenician city-state were governor and commander of the army. Details regarding the duties of these offices are sparse. However, it is known that the governor was responsible for collecting taxes, implementing decrees, supervising judges, and ensuring the administration of law and justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_46-4"><a href="#cite_note-:02-46">[46]</a></sup> As warfare was rare among the most mercantile Phoenicians, the army's commander was generally responsible for ensuring the defense and security of the city-state and its hinterlands.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2349" data-file-width="3641" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg/220px-PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg/330px-PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg/440px-PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PhoenicianStela-4cBC-Tyre-NationalMuseumOfBeirut_03102019RomanDeckert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stela" title="Stela">Stela</a> from Tyre with Phoenician inscriptions (c. 4th century BC). National Museum of Beirut.</div></div></div>
<p>The Phoenicians had a system of courts and judges that resolved disputes and punished crimes based on a semi-codified body of laws and traditions. Laws were implemented by the state and were the responsibility of the ruler and certain designated officials. Like other Levantine societies, laws were harsh and biased, reflecting the social stratification of society. The murder of a commoner was treated as less severe than that of a nobleman, and the upper classes had the most rights; the wealthy often escaped punishment by paying a fine. Free men of any class could represent themselves in court and had more rights than women and children, while slaves had no rights. Men could often deflect punishment to their wives, children, or slaves, even having them serve their sentence in their place. Lawyers eventually emerged as a profession for those who could not plead their case.
</p><p>As in neighboring societies at the time, penalties for crimes were often severe, usually reflecting the principle of reciprocity; for example, the killing of a slave would be punished by having the offender's slave killed. Imprisonment was rare, with fines, exile, punishment, and execution the main remedies.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As with most aspects of Phoenician civilization, there are few records of their military or approach to warfare. Compared to most of their neighbors, the Phoenicians generally had little interest in conquest and were relatively peaceful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The wealth and prosperity of all their city-states rested on foreign trade, which required good relations and a certain degree of mutual trust. They also lacked the territory and agricultural base to support a population large enough to raise an army of conquest. Instead, each city had an army commander in charge of a defensive garrison. However, the specifics of the role, or city defense, are unknown.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician language</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punic" title="Punic">Punic</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician language</a> was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite</a> branch of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic languages</a>. Its descendant language spoken in the Carthaginian Empire is termed <a href="/wiki/Punic_language" title="Punic language">Punic</a>. Punic was still spoken in the fifth century AD and known to St. <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alphabet">Alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Alphabet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3656" data-file-width="5299" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg/220px-Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg/330px-Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg/440px-Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ahiram_sarcophag_from_Biblos_XIII-XBC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sarcophagus of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahiram" title="Ahiram">Ahiram</a>, which bears the oldest inscription of the Phoenician alphabet. <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Beirut" title="National Museum of Beirut">National Museum of Beirut</a></div></div></div>
<p>Around 1050 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NavehJ1987_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-NavehJ1987-35">[35]</a></sup> the Phoenicians developed a script for writing <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">their own language</a>. The Canaanite-Phoenician alphabet consists of 22 letters, all <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> (and is thus strictly an <a href="/wiki/Abjad" title="Abjad">abjad</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fischer_2004_90_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fischer_2004_90-20">[20]</a></sup> It is believed to be a continuation of the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Sinaitic_script" title="Proto-Sinaitic script">Proto-Sinaitic (or Proto-Canaanite) script</a> attested in the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age" title="Late Bronze Age">Late Bronze Age</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cross1980_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cross1980-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Through their maritime trade, the Phoenicians spread the use of the alphabet to <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, North Africa, and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_C._Scott_CCR_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-John_C._Scott_CCR-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The name <i>Phoenician</i> is by convention given to inscriptions beginning around 1050 BC, because <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_languages" title="Canaanite languages">Canaanite dialects</a> were largely indistinguishable before that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NavehJ1987_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-NavehJ1987-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkoe2000111_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkoe2000111-19">[19]</a></sup> Phoenician inscriptions are found in Lebanon, Syria, Israel, Palestine, Cyprus and other locations, as late as the early centuries of the Christian era.
</p><p>The alphabet was adopted and modified by the Greeks probably in the eighth century BC. This most likely did not occur in a single instance but the process of commercial exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoscati1965_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoscati1965-107">[107]</a></sup> The legendary Phoenician hero <a href="/wiki/Cadmus" title="Cadmus">Cadmus</a> is credited with bringing the alphabet to Greece. However, it is more plausible that Phoenician immigrants brought it to <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> whence it gradually diffused northwards.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_metal_bowls" title="Phoenician metal bowls">Phoenician metal bowls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nimrud_ivories" title="Nimrud ivories">Nimrud ivories</a></div>
<p>Phoenician art was largely centered on ornamental objects, particularly jewelry, pottery, glassware, and reliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phoenician_Art_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phoenician_Art-109">[109]</a></sup> Large sculptures were rare; figurines were more common. Phoenician goods have been found from Spain and Morocco to Russia and Iraq; much of what is known about Phoenician art is based on excavations outside Phoenicia proper. Phoenician art was highly influenced by many cultures, primarily Egypt, Greece, and Assyria. Greek inspiration was particularly pronounced in pottery, while Egyptian styles were most reflected in ivory work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phoenician_Art_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phoenician_Art-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Phoenician art also differed from its contemporaries in its continuance of <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> conventions well into the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, such as terracotta masks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-110">[110]</a></sup> Phoenician artisans were known for their skill with wood, ivory, bronze, and textiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, a craftsman from Tyre is commissioned to build and decorate the legendary <a href="/wiki/Solomon%27s_Temple" title="Solomon's Temple">Solomon's Temple</a> in Jerusalem, which "presupposes a well-developed and highly respected craft industry in Phoenicia by the mid-tenth century BC".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-110">[110]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> mentions the embroidered robes of <a href="/wiki/Priam" title="Priam">Priam</a>’s wife, Hecabe, as "the work of Sidonian women" and describes a mixing bowl of <a href="/wiki/Repouss%C3%A9_and_chasing" title="Repoussé and chasing">chased silver</a> as "a masterpiece of Sidonian craftsmanship."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Assyrians appeared to have valued Phoenician ivory work in particular, collecting vast quantities in their palaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Phoenician art appears to have been indelibly tied to Phoenician commercial interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-:42-79">[79]</a></sup> They have crafted goods to appeal to particular trading partners, distinguishing not only different cultures but even socioeconomic status classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-:42-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">

</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glass_face_bead_MET_DP121044.jpg" title="Face bead; mid-4th–3rd century BC; glass; height: 2.7  cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art"><img alt="Face bead; mid-4th–3rd century BC; glass; height: 2.7  cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art" data-file-height="3842" data-file-width="2882" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Glass_face_bead_MET_DP121044.jpg/127px-Glass_face_bead_MET_DP121044.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Glass_face_bead_MET_DP121044.jpg/191px-Glass_face_bead_MET_DP121044.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Glass_face_bead_MET_DP121044.jpg/255px-Glass_face_bead_MET_DP121044.jpg 2x" width="127"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Face bead; mid-4th–3rd century BC; glass; height: 2.7  cm; Metropolitan Museum of Art
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">

</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women">Women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2299" data-file-width="3207" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg/220px-FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg/330px-FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg/440px-FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FemaleFigurines-Tyre-IronAge-II_NationalMuseumOfBeirut_06102019.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Female figurines from Tyre (c.1000–550 BC). National Museum of Beirut.</div></div></div>
<p>Women in Phoenicia took part in public events and religious processions, with depictions of banquets showing them casually sitting or reclining with men, dancing, and playing music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolst201131_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolst201131-113">[113]</a></sup> In most contexts, however, women were expected to dress and behave more modestly than men; female figures are almost always portrayed as draped from head to feet, with the arms sometimes covered as well.
</p><p>Although they rarely had political power, women took part in community affairs. They had some voice in the popular assemblies that began to emerge in some city-states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolst201144_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolst201144-114">[114]</a></sup> At least one woman, Unmiashtart, is recorded to have ruled Sidon in the fifth century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_46-5"><a href="#cite_note-:02-46">[46]</a></sup> The two most famous Phoenician women are political figures: <a href="/wiki/Jezebel" title="Jezebel">Jezebel</a>, portrayed in the Bible as the assertive princess of Sidon, and <a href="/wiki/Dido" title="Dido">Dido</a>, the semi-legendary founder and first queen of Carthage. In <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>'s epic poem, the <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, Dido is described as having been the co-ruler of Tyre, using cleverness to escape the tyranny of her brother Pygmalion and to secure an ideal site for Carthage.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_religion" title="Canaanite religion">Canaanite religion</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sanchuniathon" title="Sanchuniathon">Sanchuniathon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1658" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg/170px-Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg/255px-Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg/340px-Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baal_Ugarit_Louvre_AO17330.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Figure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ba%27al" title="Ba'al">Ba'al</a> with raised arm, 14th–12th century BC, found at ancient <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a> (<i>Ras Shamra</i> site), a city at the far north of the Phoenician coast. <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Musée du Louvre</a></div></div></div>
<p>The religious practices and beliefs of Phoenicia were generally common to those of their neighbors in <a href="/wiki/Canaanite_religion" title="Canaanite religion">Canaan</a>, which in turn shared characteristics common throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Semitic_religions" title="Ancient Semitic religions">ancient Semitic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoscati1957e.g.,_p._40_&amp;_113_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoscati1957e.g.,_p._40_&amp;_113-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith19561–15_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith19561–15-116">[116]</a></sup> Religious rites were primarily for city-state purposes; payment of taxes by citizens was considered in the category of religious sacrifices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaster1965113–143,_114–5_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaster1965113–143,_114–5-117">[117]</a></sup> Unfortunately, the Phoenician sacred writings known to the ancients have been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarden196283–4_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarden196283–4-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several Canaanite practices are attested in ancient sources and mentioned by scholars, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_prostitution" title="Temple prostitution">temple prostitution</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970512–513_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970512–513-119">[119]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Child_sacrifice" title="Child sacrifice">child sacrifice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970448_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970448-120">[120]</a></sup> Special sites known as "Tophets" were allegedly used by the Phoenicians "to burn their sons and their daughters in the fire," and are condemned by Yahweh in the Hebrew Bible, particularly in <i><a href="/wiki/Jeremiah" title="Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a></i> 7:30–32, and in <i><a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">2nd Kings</a></i> 23:10 and 17:17. Notwithstanding these and other important differences, cultural and religious similarities persisted between the ancient Hebrews and the Phoenicians. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970173_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970173-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Canaanite_religion" title="Canaanite religion">Canaanite religious mythology</a> does not appear as elaborate as their Semitic cousins in Mesopotamia. In Canaan the supreme god was called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_(god)" title="El (god)">El</a> (𐤀𐤋, "god").<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970258_("El")_122-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970258_("El")-122'>[122]</a></sup> The son of El was <a href="/wiki/Baal" title="Baal">Baal</a> (𐤁𐤏𐤋, "master", "lord"), a powerful <a href="/wiki/Dying-and-rising_deity" title="Dying-and-rising deity">dying-and-rising</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storm_god" title="Storm god">storm god</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Other gods were called by royal titles, such as <a href="/wiki/Melqart" title="Melqart">Melqart</a>, meaning "king of the city",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoscati1957113–4_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoscati1957113–4-124">[124]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Adonis" title="Adonis">Adonis</a> for "lord".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandon197029–30_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandon197029–30-125">[125]</a></sup> Such epithets may often have been merely local titles for the same deities.
</p><p>The Semitic pantheon was well-populated; which god became primary evidently depended on the exigencies of a particular city-state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970173,_501_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandon1970173,_501-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Melqart was prominent throughout Phoenicia and overseas, as was <a href="/wiki/Astarte" title="Astarte">Astarte</a>, a fertility goddess with regal and matronly aspects.
</p><p>Religious institutions in Tyre called <span title="Phoenician-language text"><i lang="phn">marzeh</i></span> (𐤌𐤓𐤆𐤄, "place of reunion"), did much to foster social bonding and "kin" loyalty. <span title="Phoenician-language text"><i lang="phn">Marzeh</i></span> held banquets for their membership on festival days, and many developed into elite <a href="/wiki/Fraternity" title="Fraternity">fraternities</a>. Each <span title="Phoenician-language text"><i lang="phn">marzeh</i></span> nurtured congeniality and community through a series of ritual meals shared among trusted kin in honor of deified ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkoe2000120_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkoe2000120-128">[128]</a></sup> In Carthage, which had developed a complex republican system of government, the <span title="Phoenician-language text"><i lang="phn">marzeh</i></span> may have played a role in forging social and political ties among citizens; Carthaginians were divided into different institutions that were solidified through communal feasts and banquets. Such festival groups may also have composed the voting cohort for selecting members of the city-state's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Punic_era_Tunisia#Constitution_of_State" title="History of Punic era Tunisia">Assembly</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarmington1964148_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarmington1964148-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith195633–43_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith195633–43-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Phoenicians made votive offerings to their gods, namely in the form of figurines and pottery vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-:21-131">[131]</a></sup> Hundreds of figurines and fragments have been recovered from the Mediterranean, often spanning centuries between them, suggesting they were cast into the sea to ensure safe travels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:21_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-:21-131">[131]</a></sup> Since the Phoenicians were predominantly seafaring people, it is speculated that many of their rituals were performed at sea or aboard ships. However, the specific nature of these practices is unknown.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Phoenicia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li>Cerqueiro, Daniel, <i><span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">Las Naves de Tarshis o quiénes fueron los Fenicios</i></span></i>, Buenos Aires, Ed. Peq. <li>Cioffi, Robert L., "A Palm Tree, a Colour and a Mythical Bird" (review of Josephine Quinn, <i>In Search of the Phoenicians</i>, Princeton, 2017, 360 pp., <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978%2B0%2B691%2B17527%2B0" title="Special:BookSources/978+0+691+17527+0">978 0 691 17527 0</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/London_Review_of_Books" title="London Review of Books">London Review of Books</a></i>, vol. 41, no. 1 (3 January 2019), pp. 15–16.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Thiollet" title="Jean-Pierre Thiollet">Thiollet, Jean-Pierre</a>, <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Je m'appelle Byblos</i></span></i>, foreword by Guy Gay-Para, H &amp; D, Paris, 2005, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/2-914266-04-9" title="Special:BookSources/2-914266-04-9">2-914266-04-9</a>.</li>

Harlow, Essex: Longman. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-582-49274-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-582-49274-5"><bdi>978-0-582-49274-5</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+South+West+to+AD+1%2C000+%28Regional+history+of+England+series+No.%3A8%29&amp;rft.place=Harlow%2C+Essex&amp;rft.pub=Longman&amp;rft.date=1987&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-582-49274-5&amp;rft.aulast=Todd&amp;rft.aufirst=Malcolm&amp;rft.au=Andrew+Fleming&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APhoenicia"></span>, for a critical examination of the evidence of Phoenician trade with the South West of the U.K.</li>
